{"nick":"joepie91","message":"methodism: So the problem with these \"build <thing> using <technologies>\" tutorials, is that they're not designed to *teach* you things - they're designed to walk you through the process step-by-step. That means that at the end you'll feel accomplished, but you've never actually *developed* or *learned* anything, you just followed the instructions. It's like the difference between 'designing furniture' and 'assembling an IKEA wardrobe' -- the latter","date":"2017-06-20T18:58:41.014Z","type":"message"}
{"nick":"joepie91","message":"won't ever teach you more than a basic sense of how to combine objects, and it'll be almost entirely useless when what you really want to learn or do is 'designing furniture'. If you want to actually learn to design furniture (ie. develop software), then you need to develop the ability to break a goal into multiple requirements or problems, and solve them individually and glue them together in a coherent way - in a way that is *specific to your","date":"2017-06-20T18:58:42.484Z","type":"message"}
{"nick":"joepie91","message":"project*, instead of specific to that of somebody else. For that reason, it's also a bad idea to start with somebody elses tutorial and then add on your own things; the underlying design of your project will not be suitable for *your* goal, it'll only be suitable for the goal of whoever made the tutorial, and the two are almost never the same in programming.","date":"2017-06-20T18:58:44.094Z","type":"message"}
